<p><b>Michael
Joseph
Jackson</b><sup
id="cite_ref-Legal_name_0-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Legal_name-0">[1]</a></sup>
(August
29,
1958
–
June
25,
2009)
was
an
American
<a
href="/wiki/Recording_artist?qsrc=3044"
title="Recording
artist">recording
artist</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Entertainer?qsrc=3044"
title="Entertainer">entertainer</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Businessman?qsrc=3044"
title="Businessman">businessman</a>.
Often
referred
to
as
the
<a
href="/wiki/Honorific_nicknames_in_popular_music?qsrc=3044"
title="Honorific
nicknames
in
popular
music">King
of
Pop</a>,
or
by
his
initials
<b>MJ</b>,<sup
id="cite_ref-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-1">[2]</a></sup>
Jackson
is
recognized
as
the
most
successful
entertainer
of
all
time
by
<a
href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records?qsrc=3044"
title="Guinness
World
Records">Guinness
World
Records</a>.
His
unparalleled
contributions
to
music,
dance,
and
fashion,
along
with
a
much-publicized
personal
life,
made
him
a
global
figure
in
<a
href="/wiki/Popular_culture?qsrc=3044"
title="Popular
culture">popular
culture</a>
for
over
four
decades.
The
eighth
child
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/Jackson_family?qsrc=3044"
title="Jackson
family">Jackson
family</a>,
he
debuted
on
the
professional
music
scene
along
with
his
brothers
as
a
member
of
<a
href="/wiki/The_Jackson_5?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Jackson
5">The
Jackson
5</a>
in
1964,
and
began
his
solo
career
in
1971.</p>








<p>In
the
early
1980s,
Jackson
became
a
dominant
figure
in
<a
href="/wiki/Popular_music?qsrc=3044"
title="Popular
music">popular
music</a>.
The
music
videos
for
his
songs,
including
those
of
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Beat_It?qsrc=3044"
title="Beat
It">Beat
It</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Billie_Jean?qsrc=3044"
title="Billie
Jean">Billie
Jean</a>&quot;,
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Thriller_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Thriller
(song)">Thriller</a>&quot;,
were
credited
with
breaking
down
racial
barriers
and
transforming
the
medium
into
an
art
form
and
promotional
tool.
The
popularity
of
these
videos
helped
to
bring
the
then
relatively
new
television
channel
<a
href="/wiki/MTV?qsrc=3044"
title="MTV">MTV</a>
to
fame.
With
videos
such
as
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Black_or_White?qsrc=3044"
title="Black
or
White">Black
or
White</a>&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Scream/Childhood?qsrc=3044"
title="Scream/Childhood">Scream</a>&quot;
he
continued
to
innovate
the
medium
throughout
the
1990s,
as
well
as
forging
a
reputation
as
a
touring
solo
artist.
Through
stage
and
video
performances,
Jackson
popularized
a
number
of
complicated
dance
techniques,
such
as
the
<a
href="/wiki/Robot_(dance)?qsrc=3044"
title="Robot
(dance)">robot</a>,
and
the
<a
href="/wiki/Moonwalk_(dance)?qsrc=3044"
title="Moonwalk
(dance)">moonwalk</a>,
to
which
he
gave
the
name.
His
distinctive
sound
and
style
has
influenced
numerous
<a
href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music?qsrc=3044"
title="Hip
hop
music">hip
hop</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Post-disco?qsrc=3044"
title="Post-disco">post-disco</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Contemporary_R%26B?qsrc=3044"
title="Contemporary
R&amp;B">contemporary
R&amp;B</a>,
pop
and
rock
artists.</p>








<p>Jackson's
1982
album
<i><a
href="/wiki/Thriller_(album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Thriller
(album)">Thriller</a></i>
is
the
<a
href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_albums?qsrc=3044"
title="List
of
best-selling
albums">best-selling
album
of
all
time</a>.
His
other
records,
including
<i><a
href="/wiki/Off_the_Wall_(album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Off
the
Wall
(album)">Off
the
Wall</a></i>
(1979),
<i><a
href="/wiki/Bad_(album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Bad
(album)">Bad</a></i>
(1987),
<i><a
href="/wiki/Dangerous_(Michael_Jackson_album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Dangerous
(Michael
Jackson
album)">Dangerous</a></i>
(1991),
and
<i><a
href="/wiki/HIStory:_Past,_Present_and_Future,_Book_I?qsrc=3044"
title="HIStory:
Past,
Present
and
Future,
Book
I">HIStory</a></i>
(1995),
also
rank
among
the
world's
best-selling.
Jackson
is
one
of
the
few
artists
to
have
been
inducted
into
the
<a
href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_Hall_of_Fame?qsrc=3044"
title="Rock
and
Roll
Hall
of
Fame">Rock
and
Roll
Hall
of
Fame</a>
twice.
He
was
also
inducted
into
the
Dance
Hall
of
Fame
as
the
first
and
only
dancer
from
pop
and
rock
music.
Some
of
his
other
<a
href="/wiki/Records_and_achievements_of_Michael_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Records
and
achievements
of
Michael
Jackson">achievements</a>
include
multiple
Guinness
World
Records;
13
<a
href="/wiki/Grammy_Award?qsrc=3044"
title="Grammy
Award">Grammy
Awards</a>
as
well
as
the
<a
href="/wiki/Grammy_Legend_Award?qsrc=3044"
title="Grammy
Legend
Award">Grammy
Legend
Award</a>
and
the
<a
href="/wiki/Grammy_Lifetime_Achievement_Award?qsrc=3044"
title="Grammy
Lifetime
Achievement
Award">Grammy
Lifetime
Achievement
Award</a>;
26
<a
href="/wiki/American_Music_Awards?qsrc=3044"
title="American
Music
Awards">American
Music
Awards</a>,
more
than
any
other
artist,
including
the
&quot;Artist
of
the
Century&quot;
and
&quot;Artist
of
the
1980s&quot;;
13
<a
href="/wiki/List_of_artists_who_reached_number_one_in_the_United_States?qsrc=3044#J"
title="List
of
artists
who
reached
number
one
in
the
United
States">number-one
singles
in
the
United
States</a>
in
his
solo
career,
more
than
any
other
male
artist
in
the
<a
href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100?qsrc=3044"
title="Billboard
Hot
100">Hot
100</a>
era;
and
the
estimated
sale
of
over
750&nbsp;million
records
worldwide.
Jackson
won
<a
href="/wiki/List_of_awards_received_by_Michael_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="List
of
awards
received
by
Michael
Jackson">hundreds
of
awards</a>,
which
made
him
the
most-awarded
recording
artist
in
the
history
of
popular
music.<sup
id="cite_ref-20_People_Who_Changed_Black_Music:_Michael_Jackson.2C_the_Child_Star-Turned-Adult_Enigma_2-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-20_People_Who_Changed_Black_Music:_Michael_Jackson.2C_the_Child_Star-Turned-Adult_Enigma-2">[3]</a></sup>
He
was
also
one
of
the
world's
most
prominent
humanitarians
and
philanthropists;
personally,
and
through
his
<a
href="/wiki/Heal_the_World_Foundation?qsrc=3044"
title="Heal
the
World
Foundation">Heal
the
World
Foundation</a>,
he
donated
more
than
$300
million
in
charity,
holds
the
<a
href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records?qsrc=3044"
title="Guinness
World
Records">Guinness
World
Record</a>
for
having
supported
the
most
charities
out
of
any
pop
star,
and
echoed
these
themes
throughout
some
of
his
notable
songs.<sup
id="cite_ref-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-3">[4]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-4">[5]</a></sup></p>








<p>Aspects
of
Jackson's
personal
life,
including
his
changing
<a
href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson%27s_health_and_appearance?qsrc=3044"
title="Michael
Jackson's
health
and
appearance">appearance</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Personal_relationships_of_Michael_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Personal
relationships
of
Michael
Jackson">personal
relationships</a>,
and
behavior,
generated
controversy.
In
1993,
he
was
<a
href="/wiki/1993_child_sexual_abuse_accusations_against_Michael_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="1993
child
sexual
abuse
accusations
against
Michael
Jackson">accused
of
child
sexual
abuse</a>,
but
the
case
was
settled
out
of
court
and
no
formal
charges
were
brought.
In
2005,
he
was
<a
href="/wiki/People_v._Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="People
v.
Jackson">tried
and
acquitted</a>
of
further
child
sexual
abuse
allegations
and
several
other
charges
after
the
jury
found
him
not
guilty
on
all
counts.
While
preparing
for
his
concert
series
titled
<i><a
href="/wiki/This_Is_It_(Michael_Jackson_concerts)?qsrc=3044"
title="This
Is
It
(Michael
Jackson
concerts)">This
Is
It</a></i>,
Jackson
<a
href="/wiki/Death_of_Michael_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Death
of
Michael
Jackson">died</a>
of
acute
<a
href="/wiki/Propofol?qsrc=3044"
title="Propofol">propofol</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine?qsrc=3044"
title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepine</a>
intoxication
on
June
25,
2009,
after
suffering
from
cardiac
arrest.
The
<a
href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_County_Coroner%27s_Office?qsrc=3044"
title="Los
Angeles
County
Coroner's
Office">Los
Angeles
County
Coroner</a>
ruled
his
death
a
homicide,
and
<a
href="/wiki/Trial_of_Conrad_Murray?qsrc=3044"
title="Trial
of
Conrad
Murray">his
personal
physician</a>
was
convicted
of
<a
href="/wiki/Involuntary_manslaughter?qsrc=3044"
title="Involuntary
manslaughter">involuntary
manslaughter</a>.
Jackson's
death
triggered
a
global
outpouring
of
grief,
and
as
many
as
one
billion
people
around
the
world
reportedly
watched
his
<a
href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson_memorial_service?qsrc=3044"
title="Michael
Jackson
memorial
service">public
memorial
service</a>
on
live
television.<sup
class="Template-Fact"
style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed"
title="Wikipedia:Citation
needed"><span
title="This
claim
needs
references
to
reliable
sources
from
October
2012">citation
needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
In
March
2010,
<a
href="/wiki/Sony_Music_Entertainment?qsrc=3044"
title="Sony
Music
Entertainment">Sony
Music
Entertainment</a>
and
Jackson's
estate
signed
the
largest
music
contract
ever,
with
a
$250
million
deal
to
retain
distribution
rights
to
his
recordings
until
2017,
and
to
release
seven
posthumous
albums
over
the
decade
following
his
death.<sup
id="cite_ref-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-5">[6]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Life_and_career">Life
and
career</span></h2>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Early_life_and_The_Jackson_5_.281958.E2.80.931975.29">Early
life
and
The
Jackson
5
(1958–1975)</span></h3>








<div
class="thumb
tright">









<div
class="thumbinner"
style="width:182px;">









<a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2300_Jackson_Street_Yuksel.jpg"
class="image"><img
alt="A
house
surrounded
by
yellow
colored
grass,
flowers,
trees,
and
a
light
blue
colored
sky
can
be
seen.
The
house
has
white
walls,
two
windows,
a
white
door
with
a
black
door
frame,
and
a
black
roof.
In
front
of
the
house
there
is
a
walk
way,
yellow
grass
and
multiple
colored
flowers
and
memorabilia.
In
the
background,
there
are
two
tall
trees
and
a
light
blue
colored
sky
that
has
multiple
clouds."
src="http://rpmedia.ask.com:80/ts?u=/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/2300_Jackson_Street_Yuksel.jpg/180px-2300_Jackson_Street_Yuksel.jpg"
width="180"
height="135"
class="thumbimage"
/></a>










<div
class="thumbcaption">











<div
class="magnify">











<a
href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:2300_Jackson_Street_Yuksel.jpg"
class="internal"
title="Enlarge"><img
src="/images/wikipedia/magnify-clip.png"
width="15"
height="11"
alt=""
/></a>










</div>
Jackson's
childhood
home
in











<a
href="/wiki/Gary,_Indiana?qsrc=3044"
title="Gary,
Indiana">Gary,
Indiana</a>,
showing
floral
tributes
after
his
death









</div>









</div>








</div>








<p>Michael
Jackson
was
born
on
August
29,
1958,
the
eighth
of
ten
children
in
an
African
American
working-class
family
who
lived
in
a
small
3-room
house
in
<a
href="/wiki/Gary,_Indiana?qsrc=3044"
title="Gary,
Indiana">Gary,
Indiana</a>,<sup
id="cite_ref-jackson_6-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-jackson-6">[7]</a></sup>
an
industrial
city
near
Chicago.
His
mother,
<a
href="/wiki/Katherine_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Katherine
Jackson">Katherine
Esther
Scruse</a>,
was
a
devout
<a
href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witness?qsrc=3044"
title="Jehovah's
Witness">Jehovah's
Witness</a>,
and
his
father,
<a
href="/wiki/Joe_Jackson_(manager)?qsrc=3044"
title="Joe
Jackson
(manager)">Joseph
Walter
&quot;Joe&quot;
Jackson</a>,
was
a
steel
mill
worker
who
performed
with
an
R&amp;B
band
called
The
Falcons.
Jackson
had
three
sisters:
<a
href="/wiki/Rebbie_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Rebbie
Jackson">Rebbie</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/La_Toya_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="La
Toya
Jackson">La
Toya</a>,
and
<a
href="/wiki/Janet_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Janet
Jackson">Janet</a>,
and
five
brothers:
<a
href="/wiki/Jackie_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Jackie
Jackson">Jackie</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Tito_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Tito
Jackson">Tito</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Jermaine_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Jermaine
Jackson">Jermaine</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Marlon_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Marlon
Jackson">Marlon</a>,
and
<a
href="/wiki/Randy_Jackson_(The_Jacksons)?qsrc=3044"
title="Randy
Jackson
(The
Jacksons)">Randy</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-Nelson_George_overview_20_7-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nelson_George_overview_20-7">[8]</a></sup>
A
sixth
brother,
Brandon,
died
shortly
after
birth.<sup
id="cite_ref-taraborrelli_8-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-taraborrelli-8">[9]</a></sup></p>








<p>Jackson
had
a
troubled
relationship
with
his
father,
Joe.<sup
id="cite_ref-MJ.27s_secret_childhood_9-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-MJ.27s_secret_childhood-9">[10]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-tara_20-22_10-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_20-22-10">[11]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_11-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-11">[12]</a></sup>
Joseph
acknowledged
in
2003
that
he
regularly
whipped
Jackson
as
a
boy.<sup
id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_11-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-11">[12]</a></sup>
Jackson
stated
that
he
was
physically
and
emotionally
abused
during
incessant
rehearsals,
though
he
also
credited
his
father's
strict
discipline
with
playing
a
large
role
in
his
success.<sup
id="cite_ref-MJ.27s_secret_childhood_9-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-MJ.27s_secret_childhood-9">[10]</a></sup>
Jackson
first
spoke
openly
about
his
childhood
abuse
in
an
interview
with
<a
href="/wiki/Oprah_Winfrey?qsrc=3044"
title="Oprah
Winfrey">Oprah
Winfrey</a>,
broadcast
in
February
1993.
He
admitted
that
he
had
often
cried
from
loneliness
and
he
would
vomit
on
the
sight
of
his
father.
Jackson's
father
was
also
said
to
have
verbally
abused
Jackson,
saying
that
he
had
a
fat
nose
on
numerous
occasions.<sup
id="cite_ref-bbc_12-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-bbc-12">[13]</a></sup>
In
fact,
Michael
Jackson's
deep
dissatisfaction
with
his
appearance,
his
nightmares
and
chronic
sleep
problems,
his
tendency
to
remain
hyper-compliant
especially
with
his
father,
and
to
remain
childlike
throughout
his
adult
life
are
in
many
ways
consistent
with
the
effects
of
this
chronic
maltreatment
he
endured
as
a
young
child.<sup
id="cite_ref-maltreatment_13-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-maltreatment-13">[14]</a></sup></p>








<p>In
an
interview
with
<a
href="/wiki/Martin_Bashir?qsrc=3044"
title="Martin
Bashir">Martin
Bashir</a>,
later
included
in
the
2003
broadcast
of
<i><a
href="/wiki/Living_with_Michael_Jackson?qsrc=3044"
title="Living
with
Michael
Jackson">Living
with
Michael
Jackson</a></i>,
Jackson
acknowledged
that
his
father
hurt
him
when
he
was
a
child,
but
was
nonetheless
a
&quot;genius&quot;,
as
he
admitted
his
father's
strict
discipline
played
a
huge
role
in
his
success.
When
Bashir
dismissed
the
positive
remark
and
continued
asking
about
beatings,
Jackson
put
his
hand
over
his
face
and
objected
to
the
questions.
He
recalled
that
Joseph
sat
in
a
chair
with
a
belt
in
his
hand
as
he
and
his
siblings
rehearsed,
and
that
&quot;if
you
didn't
do
it
the
right
way,
he
would
tear
you
up,
really
get
you&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-taraborrelli1_14-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-taraborrelli1-14">[15]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-lewis_165-168_15-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-lewis_165-168-15">[16]</a></sup></p>

class="thumb
tleft">

class="thumbimage"
/></a>










<div
class="thumbcaption">











<div
class="magnify">











<a

class="internal"
title="Enlarge"><img

/></a>










</div>
Jackson
(center)
as
a
member
of
The
Jackson
5
in
1972









</div>









</div>








</div>








<p>In
1964,
Michael
and
Marlon
joined
the
Jackson
Brothers—a
band
formed
by
brothers
Jackie,
Tito,
and
Jermaine—as
backup
musicians
playing
<a
href="/wiki/Conga?qsrc=3044"
title="Conga">congas</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Tambourine?qsrc=3044"
title="Tambourine">tambourine</a>.
Jackson
later
began
performing
backup
vocals
and
dancing.
When
he
was
eight,
Jackson
began
sharing
the
lead
vocals
with
his
older
brother
Jermaine,
and
the
group's
name
was
changed
to
<a
href="/wiki/The_Jackson_5?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Jackson
5">The
Jackson
5</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-Nelson_George_overview_20_7-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nelson_George_overview_20-7">[8]</a></sup>
The
band
toured
<a
href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States?qsrc=3044"
title="Midwestern
United
States">the
Midwest</a>
extensively
from
1966
to
1968,
frequently
performing
at
a
string
of
black
clubs
known
as
the
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Chitlin%27_circuit?qsrc=3044"
title="Chitlin'
circuit">chitlin'
circuit</a>&quot;,
where
they
often
opened
<a
href="/wiki/Striptease?qsrc=3044"
title="Striptease">stripteases</a>
and
other
adult
acts.
In
1966,
they
won
a
major
local
talent
show
with
renditions
of
<a
href="/wiki/Motown?qsrc=3044"
title="Motown">Motown</a>
hits
and
<a
href="/wiki/James_Brown?qsrc=3044"
title="James
Brown">James
Brown</a>'s
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/I_Got_You_(I_Feel_Good)?qsrc=3044"
title="I
Got
You
(I
Feel
Good)">I
Got
You
(I
Feel
Good)</a>&quot;,
led
by
Michael.<sup
id="cite_ref-RRHF_16-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-RRHF-16">[17]</a></sup></p>








<p>The
Jackson
5
recorded
several
songs,
including
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Big_Boy_(The_Jackson_5_song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Big
Boy
(The
Jackson
5
song)">Big
Boy</a>&quot;,
for
the
local
record
label
<a
href="/wiki/Steeltown_Records?qsrc=3044"
title="Steeltown
Records">Steeltown</a>
in
1967,
before
signing
with
Motown
Records
in
1968.<sup
id="cite_ref-Nelson_George_overview_20_7-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nelson_George_overview_20-7">[8]</a></sup>
<i>Rolling
Stone</i>
magazine
later
described
the
young
Michael
as
&quot;a
prodigy&quot;
with
&quot;overwhelming
musical
gifts,&quot;
writing
that
he
&quot;quickly
emerged
as
the
main
draw
and
lead
singer.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-rollingstonebio_17-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-rollingstonebio-17">[18]</a></sup>
The
group
set
a
chart
record
when
its
first
four
singles
(&quot;<a
href="/wiki/I_Want_You_Back?qsrc=3044"
title="I
Want
You
Back">I
Want
You
Back</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/ABC_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="ABC
(song)">ABC</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/The_Love_You_Save?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Love
You
Save">The
Love
You
Save</a>&quot;,
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/I%27ll_Be_There_(The_Jackson_5_song)?qsrc=3044"
title="I'll
Be
There
(The
Jackson
5
song)">I'll
Be
There</a>&quot;)
peaked
at
number
one
on
the
<a
href="/wiki/Billboard_Hot_100?qsrc=3044"
title="Billboard
Hot
100"><i>Billboard</i>
Hot
100</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-Nelson_George_overview_20_7-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nelson_George_overview_20-7">[8]</a></sup>
Between
1972
and
1975,
Michael
released
four
solo
studio
albums
with
Motown,
among
them
<i><a
href="/wiki/Got_to_Be_There?qsrc=3044"
title="Got
to
Be
There">Got
to
Be
There</a></i>
and
<i><a
href="/wiki/Ben_(album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Ben
(album)">Ben</a></i>,
released
as
part
of
the
Jackson&nbsp;5
franchise,
and
producing
successful
singles
such
as
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Got_to_Be_There_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Got
to
Be
There
(song)">Got
to
Be
There</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Ben_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Ben
(song)">Ben</a>&quot;,
and
a
remake
of
<a
href="/wiki/Bobby_Day?qsrc=3044"
title="Bobby
Day">Bobby
Day</a>'s
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Rockin%27_Robin_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Rockin'
Robin
(song)">Rockin'
Robin</a>&quot;.</p>








<p>The
Jackson
5
&quot;became
a
cutting-edge
example
of
black
crossover
artists...
five
working-class
black
boys
with
<a
href="/wiki/Afro?qsrc=3044"
title="Afro">afros</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Bell-bottoms?qsrc=3044"
title="Bell-bottoms">bell
bottoms</a>,
and
they
really
didn't
have
to
trade
any
of
that
stuff
in
order
to
become
mainstream
stars.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-Overcoming_racial_barriers_18-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Overcoming_racial_barriers-18">[19]</a></sup></p>








<p>The
group's
sales
began
declining
in
1973,
and
the
band
members
chafed
under
Motown's
strict
refusal
to
allow
them
creative
control
or
input.
Although
they
scored
several
top&nbsp;40
hits,
including
the
top&nbsp;5
<a
href="/wiki/Disco?qsrc=3044"
title="Disco">disco</a>
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Dancing_Machine?qsrc=3044"
title="Dancing
Machine">Dancing
Machine</a>&quot;
and
the
top&nbsp;20
hit
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/I_Am_Love_(song)?qsrc=3044"
title="I
Am
Love
(song)">I
Am
Love</a>&quot;,
the
Jackson&nbsp;5
left
Motown
in
1975.<sup
id="cite_ref-Nelson_George_overview_22_19-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nelson_George_overview_22-19">[20]</a></sup></p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Move_to_Epic_and_Off_the_Wall_.281975.E2.80.9381.29">Move
to
Epic
and
<i>Off
the
Wall</i>
(1975–81)</span></h3>








<p>In
June
1975,
the
Jackson
5
signed
with
<a
href="/wiki/Epic_Records?qsrc=3044"
title="Epic
Records">Epic
Records</a>,
a
subsidiary
of
<a
href="/wiki/Sony_Music_Entertainment?qsrc=3044"
title="Sony
Music
Entertainment">CBS
Records</a><sup
id="cite_ref-Nelson_George_overview_22_19-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nelson_George_overview_22-19">[20]</a></sup>
and
renamed
themselves
the
Jacksons.
Younger
brother
Randy
formally
joined
the
band
around
this
time,
while
Jermaine
left
to
pursue
a
solo
career.<sup
id="cite_ref-tara_138.E2.80.93144_20-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_138.E2.80.93144-20">[21]</a></sup>
They
continued
to
tour
internationally,
releasing
six
more
albums
between
1976
and
1984,
during
which
Michael
was
the
lead
songwriter,
writing
hits
such
as
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Shake_Your_Body_(Down_to_the_Ground)?qsrc=3044"
title="Shake
Your
Body
(Down
to
the
Ground)">Shake
Your
Body
(Down
to
the
Ground)</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/This_Place_Hotel?qsrc=3044"
title="This
Place
Hotel">This
Place
Hotel</a>&quot;,
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Can_You_Feel_It?qsrc=3044"
title="Can
You
Feel
It">Can
You
Feel
It</a>&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-RRHF_16-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-RRHF-16">[17]</a></sup>
In
1978,
he
starred
as
the
<a
href="/wiki/Scarecrow_(Oz)?qsrc=3044"
title="Scarecrow
(Oz)">scarecrow</a>
in
the
musical,
<i><a
href="/wiki/The_Wiz_(film)?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Wiz
(film)">The
Wiz</a></i>,
a
box-office
disaster.
It
was
here
that
he
teamed
up
with
<a
href="/wiki/Quincy_Jones?qsrc=3044"
title="Quincy
Jones">Quincy
Jones</a>,
who
was
arranging
the
film's
musical
score.
Jones
agreed
to
produce
Jackson's
next
solo
album,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Off_the_Wall_(album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Off
the
Wall
(album)">Off
the
Wall</a></i>.<sup
id="cite_ref-Nelson_George_overview_23_21-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nelson_George_overview_23-21">[22]</a></sup>
In
1979,
Jackson
broke
his
nose
during
a
complex
dance
routine.
His
subsequent
<a
href="/wiki/Rhinoplasty?qsrc=3044"
title="Rhinoplasty">rhinoplasty</a>
was
not
a
complete
success;
he
complained
of
breathing
difficulties
that
would
affect
his
career.
He
was
referred
to
Dr.
<a
href="/wiki/Steven_Hoefflin?qsrc=3044"
title="Steven
Hoefflin">Steven
Hoefflin</a>,
who
performed
Jackson's
second
rhinoplasty
and
subsequent
operations.<sup
id="cite_ref-tara_205.E2.80.93210_22-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_205.E2.80.93210-22">[23]</a></sup></p>








<p>Jones
and
Jackson
produced
the
<i>Off
the
Wall</i>
album
together.
Songwriters
for
the
album
included
Jackson,
<a
href="/wiki/Rod_Temperton?qsrc=3044"
title="Rod
Temperton">Rod
Temperton</a>,
<a
href="/wiki/Stevie_Wonder?qsrc=3044"
title="Stevie
Wonder">Stevie
Wonder</a>,
and
<a
href="/wiki/Paul_McCartney?qsrc=3044"
title="Paul
McCartney">Paul
McCartney</a>.
Released
in
1979,
it
was
the
first
solo
album
to
generate
four
U.S.
top&nbsp;10
hits,
including
the
chart-topping
singles
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Don%27t_Stop_%27til_You_Get_Enough?qsrc=3044"
title="Don't
Stop
'til
You
Get
Enough">Don't
Stop
'til
You
Get
Enough</a>&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Rock_with_You_(Michael_Jackson_song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Rock
with
You
(Michael
Jackson
song)">Rock
with
You</a>&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-Nelson_George_overview_37-38_23-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nelson_George_overview_37-38-23">[24]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-MichaelJacksonIsBorn_24-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-MichaelJacksonIsBorn-24">[25]</a></sup>
It
reached
number
three
on
the
<a
href="/wiki/Billboard_200?qsrc=3044"
title="Billboard
200"><i>Billboard</i>
200</a>
and
eventually
sold
over
<span
class="nowrap">20
million</span>
copies
worldwide.<sup
id="cite_ref-Off_the_Wall_.7B.7BNowrap.7C20_million.7D.7D_25-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Off_the_Wall_.7B.7BNowrap.7C20_million.7D.7D-25">[26]</a></sup>
In
1980,
Jackson
won
three
awards
at
the
<a
href="/wiki/American_Music_Awards?qsrc=3044"
title="American
Music
Awards">American
Music
Awards</a>
for
his
solo
efforts:
Favorite
Soul/R&amp;B
Album,
Favorite
Soul/R&amp;B
Male
Artist,
and
Favorite
Soul/R&amp;B
Single
for
&quot;Don't
Stop
'Til
You
Get
Enough&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-AMAs_1980_26-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-AMAs_1980-26">[27]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-AMAs_1980_2_27-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-AMAs_1980_2-27">[28]</a></sup>
That
year,
he
also
won
<a
href="/wiki/Billboard_Year-End?qsrc=3044"
title="Billboard
Year-End">Billboard
Year-End</a>
for
Top
Black
Artist
and
Top
Black
Album
and
a
Grammy
Award
for
Best
Male
R&amp;B
Vocal
Performance,
also
for
&quot;Don't
Stop
'Til
You
Get
Enough&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-Nelson_George_overview_37-38_23-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nelson_George_overview_37-38-23">[24]</a></sup>
Jackson
again
won
at
the
American
Music
Awards
in
1981
for
Favorite
Soul/R&amp;B
Album
and
Favorite
Soul/R&amp;B
Male
Artist.<sup
id="cite_ref-AMAs_1981_28-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-AMAs_1981-28">[29]</a></sup>
Despite
its
commercial
success,
Jackson
felt
<i>Off
the
Wall</i>
should
have
made
a
much
bigger
impact,
and
was
determined
to
exceed
expectations
with
his
next
release.<sup
id="cite_ref-taraborrelli2_29-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-taraborrelli2-29">[30]</a></sup>
In
1980,
he
secured
the
highest
<a
href="/wiki/Royalties?qsrc=3044"
title="Royalties">royalty</a>
rate
in
the
music
industry:
37
percent
of
wholesale
album
profit.<sup
id="cite_ref-taraborrelli3_30-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-taraborrelli3-30">[31]</a></sup></p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Thriller_and_Motown_25:_Yesterday.2C_Today.2C_Forever_.281982.E2.80.9383.29"><i>Thriller</i>
and
<i>Motown
25:
Yesterday,
Today,
Forever</i>
(1982–83)</span></h3>








<p>In
1982,
Jackson
contributed
the
song
&quot;Someone
In
the
Dark&quot;
to
the
<a
href="/wiki/E.T._the_Extra-Terrestrial_(album)?qsrc=3044"
title="E.T.
the
Extra-Terrestrial
(album)">storybook</a>
for
the
film
<i><a
href="/wiki/E.T._the_Extra-Terrestrial?qsrc=3044"
title="E.T.
the
Extra-Terrestrial">E.T.
the
Extra-Terrestrial</a></i>;
the
record
won
a
Grammy
for
Best
Recording
for
Children
in
1984.
In
the
same
year
he
won
another
seven
Grammys
and
eight
American
Music
Awards
(including
the
Award
of
Merit,
the
youngest
artist
to
win
it),
making
him
the
most
awarded
artist
in
one
night
for
both
award
shows.<sup
id="cite_ref-MJ_Grammy.27s_31-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-MJ_Grammy.27s-31">[32]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-AMAs_1984_32-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-AMAs_1984-32">[33]</a></sup>
These
awards
were
thanks
to
the
<i><a
href="/wiki/Thriller_(album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Thriller
(album)">Thriller</a></i>
album,
released
in
late
1982,
which
was
1983's
best-selling
album
worldwide<sup
id="cite_ref-Thriller_best_seller_33-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Thriller_best_seller-33">[34]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Thriller_best_seller_1_34-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Thriller_best_seller_1-34">[35]</a></sup>
and
became
the
best-selling
album
of
all
time
in
the
United
States,<sup
id="cite_ref-RIAA_certification_35-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-RIAA_certification-35">[36]</a></sup>
as
well
as
the
<a
href="/wiki/List_of_best-selling_albums_worldwide?qsrc=3044"
title="List
of
best-selling
albums
worldwide">best-selling
album
of
all
time
worldwide</a>,
selling
an
estimated
<span
class="nowrap">110
million</span>
copies
so
far.<sup
id="cite_ref-Thriller_.7B.7BNowrap.7C110_million.7D.7D_copies_36-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Thriller_.7B.7BNowrap.7C110_million.7D.7D_copies-36">[37]</a></sup>
The
album
topped
the
<a
href="/wiki/Billboard_200?qsrc=3044"
title="Billboard
200"><i>Billboard</i>
200</a>
chart
for
37
weeks
and
was
in
the
top&nbsp;10
of
the
200
for
80
consecutive
weeks.
It
was
the
first
album
to
have
seven
<i>Billboard</i>
Hot&nbsp;100
top&nbsp;10
singles,
including
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Billie_Jean?qsrc=3044"
title="Billie
Jean">Billie
Jean</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Beat_It?qsrc=3044"
title="Beat
It">Beat
It</a>&quot;,
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Wanna_Be_Startin%27_Somethin%27?qsrc=3044"
title="Wanna
Be
Startin'
Somethin'">Wanna
Be
Startin'
Somethin'</a>&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-lewis_37-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-lewis-37">[38]</a></sup>
<i>Thriller</i>
was
certified
for
29&nbsp;million
shipments
by
the
<a
href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America?qsrc=3044"
title="Recording
Industry
Association
of
America">RIAA</a>,
giving
it
<a
href="/wiki/List_of_music_recording_certifications?qsrc=3044"
title="List
of
music
recording
certifications">Double
Diamond</a>
status
in
the
United
States.
The
album
won
also
another
Grammy
for
Best
Engineered
Recording&nbsp;–
Non
Classical
in
1984,
awarding
<a
href="/wiki/Bruce_Swedien?qsrc=3044"
title="Bruce
Swedien">Bruce
Swedien</a>
for
his
work.<sup
id="cite_ref-Bruce_Grammy.27s_38-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Bruce_Grammy.27s-38">[39]</a></sup></p>








<p>Jackson's
attorney
<a
href="/wiki/John_Branca?qsrc=3044"
title="John
Branca">John
Branca</a>
noted
that
Jackson
had
the
highest
royalty
rate
in
the
music
industry
at
that
point:
approximately
$2
for
every
album
sold.
He
was
also
making
record-breaking
profits
from
sales
of
his
recordings.
The
videocassette
of
the
documentary
<i>The
Making
of
Michael
Jackson's
Thriller</i>
sold
over
350,000
copies
in
a
few
months.
The
era
saw
the
arrival
of
novelties
like
dolls
modeled
after
Michael
Jackson,
which
appeared
in
stores
in
May
1984
at
a
price
of
$12.<sup
id="cite_ref-TIME_39-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-TIME-39">[40]</a></sup>
Biographer
<a
href="/wiki/J._Randy_Taraborrelli?qsrc=3044"
title="J.
Randy
Taraborrelli">J.
Randy
Taraborrelli</a>
writes
that,
&quot;<i>Thriller</i>
stopped
selling
like
a
leisure
item—like
a
magazine,
a
toy,
tickets
to
a
hit
movie—and
started
selling
like
a
household
staple.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-tara_226_40-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_226-40">[41]</a></sup>
In
1985,
<i>The
Making
of
Michael
Jackson's
Thriller</i>
won
a
Grammy
for
Best
Music
Video,
Longform.<sup
id="cite_ref-MJ_Grammy.27s_31-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-MJ_Grammy.27s-31">[32]</a></sup>
In
December
2009,
the
music
video
for
&quot;Thriller&quot;
was
selected
for
the
<a
href="/wiki/National_Film_Registry?qsrc=3044"
title="National
Film
Registry">National
Film
Registry</a>
by
the
<a
href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress?qsrc=3044"
title="Library
of
Congress">Library
of
Congress</a>,
&quot;Thriller&quot;
is
the
first
(and
currently
only)
music
video
ever
to
be
inducted.<sup
id="cite_ref-Michael_Jackson_Thriller_Induction_41-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Michael_Jackson_Thriller_Induction-41">[42]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Reuters:_U.S._registry_2009-12-30_42-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Reuters:_U.S._registry_2009-12-30-42">[43]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-.27Thriller.27_Video_Added_to_U.S._Film_Registry_43-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-.27Thriller.27_Video_Added_to_U.S._Film_Registry-43">[44]</a></sup></p>








<p><i>Time</i>
described
Jackson's
influence
at
that
point
as
&quot;Star
of
records,
radio,
rock
video.
A
one-man
rescue
team
for
the
music
business.
A
songwriter
who
sets
the
beat
for
a
decade.
A
dancer
with
the
fanciest
feet
on
the
street.
A
singer
who
cuts
across
all
boundaries
of
taste
and
style
and
color
too&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-TIME_39-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-TIME-39">[40]</a></sup>
<i>The
New
York
Times</i>
wrote
that,
&quot;in
the
world
of
pop
music,
there
is
Michael
Jackson
and
there
is
everybody
else&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-NYTimes_44-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-NYTimes-44">[45]</a></sup></p>








<p>On
March
25,
1983,
Michael
Jackson
reunited
with
his
brothers
for
a
legendary
live
performance
which
was
taped
for
a
<i><a
href="/wiki/Motown_25:_Yesterday,_Today,_Forever?qsrc=3044"
title="Motown
25:
Yesterday,
Today,
Forever">Motown
25:
Yesterday,
Today,
Forever</a></i>
television
special.
The
show
aired
on
May
16,
1983,
to
an
audience
of
<span
class="nowrap">47
million</span>
viewers,
and
featured
the
Jacksons
and
a
number
of
other
Motown
stars.
It
is
best
remembered
for
Jackson's
solo
performance
of
&quot;Billie
Jean&quot;
which
gave
him
his
first
Emmy
nomination.<sup
id="cite_ref-emmys.tv_45-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-emmys.tv-45">[46]</a></sup>
Wearing
a
distinctive
black
<a
href="/wiki/Sequin?qsrc=3044"
title="Sequin">sequin</a>
jacket
and
golf
glove
decorated
with
rhinestones,
he
debuted
his
signature
dance
move,
the
<a
href="/wiki/Moonwalk_(dance)?qsrc=3044"
title="Moonwalk
(dance)">moonwalk</a>,
which
former
<a
href="/wiki/Soul_Train?qsrc=3044"
title="Soul
Train">Soul
Train</a>
dancer
and
<a
href="/wiki/Shalamar?qsrc=3044"
title="Shalamar">Shalamar</a>
member
<a
href="/wiki/Jeffrey_Daniel?qsrc=3044"
title="Jeffrey
Daniel">Jeffrey
Daniel</a>
had
taught
him
three
years
before.
The
Jacksons'
performance
drew
comparisons
to
<a
href="/wiki/Elvis_Presley?qsrc=3044"
title="Elvis
Presley">Elvis
Presley</a>'s
and
<a
href="/wiki/The_Beatles?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Beatles">The
Beatles</a>'
appearances
on
<i><a
href="/wiki/The_Ed_Sullivan_Show?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Ed
Sullivan
Show">The
Ed
Sullivan
Show</a></i>.<sup
id="cite_ref-taraborrelli4_46-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-taraborrelli4-46">[47]</a></sup>
Anna
Kisselgoff
of
<i>The
New
York
Times</i>
later
wrote,
&quot;The
moonwalk
that
he
made
famous
is
an
apt
metaphor
for
his
dance
style.
How
does
he
do
it?
As
a
technician,
he
is
a
great
illusionist,
a
genuine
mime.
His
ability
to
keep
one
leg
straight
as
he
glides
while
the
other
bends
and
seems
to
walk
requires
perfect
timing.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-Dancing_feet_of_Michael_Jackson_47-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Dancing_feet_of_Michael_Jackson-47">[48]</a></sup></p>

and
business
career
(1984–85)</span></h3>

<div

Jackson
with

<a

<p>On
January
27,
1984,
Michael
and
other
members
of
the
Jacksons
filmed
a
Pepsi
Cola
commercial,
overseen
by
executive
<a
href="/wiki/Phil_Dusenberry?qsrc=3044"
title="Phil
Dusenberry">Phil
Dusenberry</a>,<sup
id="cite_ref-nytt_48-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-nytt-48">[49]</a></sup>
from
ad
agency
<a
href="/wiki/BBDO?qsrc=3044"
title="BBDO">BBDO</a>
and
Pepsi's
Worldwide
Creative
Director,
<a
href="/wiki/Alan_Pottasch?qsrc=3044"
title="Alan
Pottasch">Alan
Pottasch</a>
at
the
<a
href="/wiki/Shrine_Auditorium?qsrc=3044"
title="Shrine
Auditorium">Shrine
Auditorium</a>
in
Los
Angeles.
In
front
of
a
full
house
of
fans
during
a
simulated
concert,
pyrotechnics
accidentally
set
Jackson's
hair
on
fire.
He
suffered
second-degree
burns
to
his
scalp.
Jackson
underwent
treatment
to
hide
the
scars
on
his
scalp,
and
he
also
had
his
third
<a
href="/wiki/Rhinoplasty?qsrc=3044"
title="Rhinoplasty">rhinoplasty</a>
shortly
thereafter.<sup
id="cite_ref-tara_205.E2.80.93210_22-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_205.E2.80.93210-22">[23]</a></sup>
Jackson
never
recovered
from
this
injury.
Pepsi
settled
out
of
court,
and
Jackson
donated
his
$1.5&nbsp;million
settlement
to
the
Brotman
Medical
Center
in
<a
href="/wiki/Culver_City,_California?qsrc=3044"
title="Culver
City,
California">Culver
City</a>,
California,
which
now
has
a
&quot;Michael
Jackson
Burn
Center&quot;
in
honor
of
his
donation.<sup
id="cite_ref-tara_279.E2.80.93287_49-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_279.E2.80.93287-49">[50]</a></sup>
Dusenberry
later
recounted
the
episode
in
his
memoir,
<i>Then
We
Set
His
Hair
on
Fire:
Insights
and
Accidents
from
a
Hall
of
Fame
Career
in
Advertising</i>.</p>








<p>On
May
14,
1984,
Jackson
was
invited
to
the
White
House
to
receive
an
award
from
President
<a
href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan?qsrc=3044"
title="Ronald
Reagan">Ronald
Reagan</a>
for
his
support
of
charities
that
helped
people
overcome
alcohol
and
drug
abuse.<sup
id="cite_ref-taraborrelli5_50-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-taraborrelli5-50">[51]</a></sup>
Jackson
won
eight
awards
during
the
Grammys
that
year.
Unlike
later
albums,
<i>Thriller</i>
did
not
have
an
official
tour
to
promote
it,
but
the
1984
<a
href="/wiki/Victory_Tour_(The_Jacksons_tour)?qsrc=3044"
title="Victory
Tour
(The
Jacksons
tour)">Victory
Tour</a>,
headlined
by
The
Jacksons,
showcased
much
of
Jackson's
new
solo
material
to
more
than
two
million
Americans.
He
donated
all
the
funds
(around
<span
class="nowrap">$8
million</span>)
raised
from
the
Victory
Tour
to
charity.<sup
id="cite_ref-taraborrelli6_51-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-taraborrelli6-51">[52]</a></sup>
He
also
co-wrote
the
charity
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/We_Are_the_World?qsrc=3044"
title="We
Are
the
World">We
Are
the
World</a>&quot;
in
1985
with
<a
href="/wiki/Lionel_Richie?qsrc=3044"
title="Lionel
Richie">Lionel
Richie</a>,
which
was
released
worldwide
to
aid
the
poor
in
the
U.S.
and
Africa.
It
became
one
of
the
best-selling
singles
of
all
time,
with
nearly
30&nbsp;million
copies
sold
and
millions
of
dollars
donated
to
famine
relief.
In
1986,
&quot;We
Are
the
World&quot;
won
four
Grammys
(one
for
Jackson
for
Song
of
the
Year).
American
Music
Award
directors
removed
the
charity
song
from
the
competition
because
they
felt
it
would
be
inappropriate,
but
recognized
it
with
two
special
honors:
one
for
the
creation
of
the
song
and
one
for
the
<a
href="/wiki/USA_for_Africa?qsrc=3044"
title="USA
for
Africa">USA
for
Africa</a>
idea.
Michael
won
the
award
for
the
creation
of
the
song.<sup
id="cite_ref-USA_for_Africa_Grammy.27s_52-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-USA_for_Africa_Grammy.27s-52">[53]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-AMAs_1986_53-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-AMAs_1986-53">[54]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-AMAs_1986_2_54-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-AMAs_1986_2-54">[55]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-taraborrelli7_55-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-taraborrelli7-55">[56]</a></sup></p>

class="thumb
tleft">

<p>In
1984,
<a
href="/wiki/ATV_Music_Publishing?qsrc=3044"
title="ATV
Music
Publishing">ATV
Music
Publishing</a>,
which
had
the
copyrights
to
nearly
4000
songs,
including
the
<a
href="/wiki/Northern_Songs?qsrc=3044"
title="Northern
Songs">Northern
Songs</a>
catalog
that
contained
the
majority
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/Lennon%E2%80%93McCartney?qsrc=3044"
title="Lennon–McCartney">Lennon–McCartney</a>
compositions
recorded
by
<a
href="/wiki/The_Beatles?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Beatles">The
Beatles</a>,
was
put
up
for
sale
by
<a
href="/wiki/Robert_Holmes_%C3%A0_Court?qsrc=3044"
title="Robert
Holmes
&agrave;
Court">Robert
Holmes
&agrave;
Court</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-hilburn_56-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-hilburn-56">[57]</a></sup>
Jackson
had
become
interested
in
owning
<a
href="/wiki/Music_catalog?qsrc=3044"
title="Music
catalog">music
catalogs</a>
after
working
with
Paul
McCartney
in
the
early
1980s.
Jackson
had
learned
McCartney
made
approximately
$40&nbsp;million
a
year
from
other
people's
songs.<sup
id="cite_ref-jdoyle_57-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-jdoyle-57">[58]</a></sup>
In
1981,<sup
id="cite_ref-tara_333-338_58-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_333-338-58">[59]</a></sup>
McCartney
was
offered
the
ATV
music
catalog
for
&pound;20
million
($40
million).<sup
id="cite_ref-jdoyle_57-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-jdoyle-57">[58]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-mcca_atv_59-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-mcca_atv-59">[60]</a></sup>
According
to
McCartney,
he
contacted
<a
href="/wiki/Yoko_Ono?qsrc=3044"
title="Yoko
Ono">Yoko
Ono</a>
about
making
a
joint
purchase
by
splitting
the
cost
equally
at
&pound;10
million
each,
but
Ono
thought
they
could
buy
it
for
&pound;5
million
each.<sup
id="cite_ref-jdoyle_57-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-jdoyle-57">[58]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-mcca_atv_59-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-mcca_atv-59">[60]</a></sup>
When
they
were
unable
to
make
the
joint
purchase,
McCartney
let
the
offer
fall
through,
not
wanting
to
be
the
sole
owner
of
the
Beatles'
songs.<sup
id="cite_ref-tara_333-338_58-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_333-338-58">[59]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-mcca_atv_59-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-mcca_atv-59">[60]</a></sup></p>








<p>According
to
a
negotiator
for
Holmes
&agrave;
Court
in
the
1984
sale,
&quot;We
had
given
Paul
McCartney
first
right
of
refusal
but
Paul
didn't
want
it
at
that
time.&quot;<sup
id="cite_ref-ch7news_60-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-ch7news-60">[61]</a></sup>
Also,
an
attorney
for
McCartney
assured
Jackson's
attorney,
John
Branca,
that
McCartney
was
not
interested
in
bidding:
McCartney
reportedly
said
&quot;It's
too
pricey&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-jdoyle_57-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-jdoyle-57">[58]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-tara_333-338_58-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_333-338-58">[59]</a></sup>
But
there
were
several
other
companies
and
investors
bidding.
In
September
1984,
Jackson
was
first
informed
about
the
sale
by
Branca
and
sent
a
bid
of
$46
million
on
November
20,
1984.<sup
id="cite_ref-hilburn_56-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-hilburn-56">[57]</a></sup>
Jackson's
agents
thought
they
had
a
deal
several
times,
but
encountered
new
bidders
or
new
areas
of
debate.<sup
id="cite_ref-hilburn_56-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-hilburn-56">[57]</a></sup>
In
May
1985,
Jackson's
team
walked
away
from
talks
after
having
spent
over
$1
million
on
four
months
of
<a
href="/wiki/Due_diligence?qsrc=3044"
title="Due
diligence">due
diligence</a>
and
on
the
negotiations.<sup
id="cite_ref-hilburn_56-3"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-hilburn-56">[57]</a></sup></p>








<p>In
June
1985,
Jackson
and
Branca
learned
that
<a
href="/wiki/Charles_Koppelman?qsrc=3044"
title="Charles
Koppelman">Charles
Koppelman</a>'s
and
Marty
Bandier's
The
Entertainment
Co.
had
made
a
tentative
agreement
with
Holmes
&agrave;
Court
to
buy
ATV
Music
for
$50
million.<sup
id="cite_ref-hilburn_56-4"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-hilburn-56">[57]</a></sup>
But
in
early
August,
Holmes
&agrave;
Court's
team
contacted
Jackson
and
talks
resumed.
Jackson
raised
his
bid
to
$47.5
million
and
it
was
accepted
because
he
could
close
the
deal
more
quickly,
having
already
completed
due
diligence
of
ATV
Music.<sup
id="cite_ref-hilburn_56-5"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-hilburn-56">[57]</a></sup>
He
also
agreed
to
visit
Holmes
&agrave;
Court
in
Australia,
where
he
would
appear
on
the
<a
href="/wiki/Channel_Seven_Perth_Telethon?qsrc=3044"
title="Channel
Seven
Perth
Telethon">Channel
Seven
Perth
Telethon</a>.<sup
id="cite_ref-hilburn_56-6"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-hilburn-56">[57]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-ch7news_60-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-ch7news-60">[61]</a></sup>
Jackson's
<a
href="/wiki/ATV_Music_Publishing?qsrc=3044#Acquisition"
title="ATV
Music
Publishing">purchase
of
ATV
Music</a>
was
finalized
August
10,
1985.<sup
id="cite_ref-hilburn_56-7"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-hilburn-56">[57]</a></sup></p>








<h3><span
class="mw-headline"
id="Appearance.2C_tabloids.2C_Bad.2C_films.2C_autobiography_and_Neverland_.281986.E2.80.9390.29">Appearance,
tabloids,
<i>Bad</i>,
films,
autobiography
and
Neverland
(1986–90)</span></h3>








<div
class="rellink
boilerplate
seealso">








See
also:









<a
href="/wiki/Michael_Jackson%27s_health_and_appearance?qsrc=3044"
title="Michael
Jackson's
health
and
appearance">Michael
Jackson's
health
and
appearance</a>







</div>








<p>Jackson's
skin
had
been
a
medium-brown
color
for
the
entire
duration
of
his
youth,
but
starting
in
the
mid-1980s,
it
gradually
grew
paler.
The
change
gained
widespread
media
coverage,
including
rumors
that
he
might
be
bleaching
his
skin.<sup
id="cite_ref-campbell_.281995.29_14-16_61-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-campbell_.281995.29_14-16-61">[62]</a></sup>
According
to
<a
href="/wiki/J._Randy_Taraborrelli?qsrc=3044"
title="J.
Randy
Taraborrelli">J.
Randy
Taraborrelli</a>'s
biography,
in
1986,
Jackson
was
diagnosed
with
<a
href="/wiki/Vitiligo?qsrc=3044"
title="Vitiligo">vitiligo</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Systemic_lupus_erythematosus?qsrc=3044"
title="Systemic
lupus
erythematosus">lupus</a>;
the
vitiligo
partially
lightened
his
skin,
and
the
lupus
was
in
<a
href="/wiki/Remission_(medicine)?qsrc=3044"
title="Remission
(medicine)">remission</a>;
both
illnesses
made
him
sensitive
to
sunlight.
The
treatments
he
used
for
his
condition
further
lightened
his
skin
tone,
and,
with
the
application
of
pancake
makeup
to
even
out
blotches,
he
could
appear
very
pale.<sup
id="cite_ref-Taraborrelli_62-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Taraborrelli-62">[63]</a></sup>
Jackson
was
also
diagnosed
with
vitiligo
in
his
autopsy.<sup
id="cite_ref-Michael_Jackson_case_report_63-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Michael_Jackson_case_report-63">[64]</a></sup>
By
the
mid-1990s
several
surgeons
speculated
that
he
had
undergone
various
nasal
surgeries,
a
forehead
lift,
thinned
lips,
and
cheekbone
surgery—although
Jackson
denied
this
and
insisted
that
he
only
had
surgery
on
his
nose.<sup
id="cite_ref-abcnews_64-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-abcnews-64">[65]</a></sup>
Jackson
claimed
that
he
had
only
two
rhinoplasties
and
no
other
surgery
on
his
face,
although
at
one
point
he
mentioned
having
a
dimple
created
in
his
chin.<sup
id="cite_ref-jackson_229-230_65-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-jackson_229-230-65">[66]</a></sup>
Jackson
lost
weight
in
the
early
1980s
because
of
a
change
in
diet
and
a
desire
for
&quot;a
dancer's
body&quot;.<sup
id="cite_ref-jackson_229-230_65-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-jackson_229-230-65">[66]</a></sup>
Witnesses
reported
that
he
was
often
dizzy
and
speculated
that
he
was
suffering
from
<a
href="/wiki/Anorexia_nervosa?qsrc=3044"
title="Anorexia
nervosa">anorexia
nervosa</a>;
periods
of
weight
loss
would
become
a
recurring
problem
later
in
life.<sup
id="cite_ref-tara_312.E2.80.93313_66-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_312.E2.80.93313-66">[67]</a></sup></p>








<p>During
the
course
of
his
treatment,
Jackson
made
two
close
friends:
his
dermatologist,
Dr.
<a
href="/wiki/Arnold_Klein?qsrc=3044"
title="Arnold
Klein">Arnold
Klein</a>,
and
Klein's
nurse
<a
href="/wiki/Debbie_Rowe?qsrc=3044"
title="Debbie
Rowe">Debbie
Rowe</a>.
Rowe
eventually
became
Jackson's
second
wife
and
the
mother
of
his
two
eldest
children.
He
also
relied
heavily
on
Klein
for
medical
and
business
advice.<sup
id="cite_ref-Michael_Jackson_Secret_Boyfriend_Claims_Draw_Fury_67-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Michael_Jackson_Secret_Boyfriend_Claims_Draw_Fury-67">[68]</a></sup></p>








<p>Jackson
became
the
subject
of
increasingly
sensational
reports.
In
1986,
the
tabloids
ran
a
story
claiming
that
Jackson
slept
in
a
<a
href="/wiki/Hyperbaric_medicine?qsrc=3044"
title="Hyperbaric
medicine">hyperbaric
oxygen
chamber</a>
to
slow
the
aging
process;
he
was
pictured
lying
down
in
a
glass
box.
Although
the
claim
was
untrue,
according
to
tabloid
reports
that
are
widely
cited,
Jackson
had
disseminated
the
fabricated
story
himself.<sup
id="cite_ref-tara_355-361_68-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_355-361-68">[69]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-BBC.2C_Jackson.27s_image_problems_69-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-BBC.2C_Jackson.27s_image_problems-69">[70]</a></sup>
When
Jackson
bought
a
chimpanzee
called
<a
href="/wiki/Bubbles_(chimpanzee)?qsrc=3044"
title="Bubbles
(chimpanzee)">Bubbles</a>
from
a
laboratory,
he
was
reported
to
be
increasingly
detached
from
reality.<sup
id="cite_ref-Is_Michael_Jackson_for_Real.3F_70-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Is_Michael_Jackson_for_Real.3F-70">[71]</a></sup>
It
was
reported
that
Jackson
had
offered
to
buy
the
bones
of
<a
href="/wiki/Joseph_Merrick?qsrc=3044"
title="Joseph
Merrick">Joseph
Merrick</a>
(the
&quot;elephant
man&quot;)
and
although
untrue,
Jackson
did
not
deny
the
story.<sup
id="cite_ref-tara_355-361_68-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_355-361-68">[69]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-BBC.2C_Jackson.27s_image_problems_69-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-BBC.2C_Jackson.27s_image_problems-69">[70]</a></sup>
Although
initially
he
saw
these
stories
as
opportunities
for
publicity,
he
stopped
leaking
untruths
to
the
press
as
they
became
more
sensational.
Consequently
the
media
began
making
up
their
own
stories.<sup
id="cite_ref-BBC.2C_Jackson.27s_image_problems_69-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-BBC.2C_Jackson.27s_image_problems-69">[70]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-tara_370.E2.80.93373_71-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-tara_370.E2.80.93373-71">[72]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Newswatch_Magazine.C2.A0.E2.80.93_The_Man.2C_His_Weird_Ways_72-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Newswatch_Magazine.C2.A0.E2.80.93_The_Man.2C_His_Weird_Ways-72">[73]</a></sup>
These
reports
became
embedded
in
the
public
consciousness,
inspiring
the
nickname
&quot;Wacko
Jacko&quot;,
which
Jackson
came
to
despise.<sup
id="cite_ref-jackson8_73-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-jackson8-73">[74]</a></sup>
Responding
to
the
gossip,
Jackson
remarked
to
Taraborrelli:</p>








<blockquote>








Why
not
just
tell
people
I'm
an
alien
from
Mars?
Tell
them
I
eat
live
chickens
and
do
a
voodoo
dance
at
midnight.
They'll
believe
anything









<i>you</i>
say,
because









<i>you're
a
reporter</i>.
But
if
I,
Michael
Jackson,
were
to
say,
&quot;I'm
an
alien
from
Mars
and
I
eat
live
chickens
and
do
a
voodoo
dance
at
midnight,&quot;
people
would
say,
&quot;Oh,
man,
that
Michael
Jackson
is









<i>nuts</i>.
He's
cracked
up.
You
can't
believe
a
single
word
that
comes
out
of
his
mouth.&quot;








<sup
id="cite_ref-taraborrelli9_74-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-taraborrelli9-74">[75]</a></sup>







</blockquote>








<div
class="thumb
tright">

class="thumbinner"
style="width:142px;">

Jackson
wore
a
gold-plated
military
style
jacket
with
belt
in
the











<i>Bad</i>
era









</div>









</div>








</div>








<p>Jackson
collaborated
with
<a
href="/wiki/George_Lucas?qsrc=3044"
title="George
Lucas">George
Lucas</a>
and
<a
href="/wiki/Francis_Ford_Coppola?qsrc=3044"
title="Francis
Ford
Coppola">Francis
Ford
Coppola</a>
on
the
17-minute
<a
href="/wiki/3-D_film?qsrc=3044"
title="3-D
film">3-D
film</a>
<i><a
href="/wiki/Captain_EO?qsrc=3044"
title="Captain
EO">Captain
EO</a></i>,
which
debuted
in
September
1986
at
both
the
original
<a
href="/wiki/Disneyland?qsrc=3044"
title="Disneyland">Disneyland</a>
and
at
<a
href="/wiki/EPCOT?qsrc=3044"
title="EPCOT">EPCOT</a>
in
Florida,
and
in
March
1987
at
<a
href="/wiki/Tokyo_Disneyland?qsrc=3044"
title="Tokyo
Disneyland">Tokyo
Disneyland</a>.
The
$30
million
movie
was
a
popular
attraction
at
all
three
parks.
A
Captain
EO
attraction
was
later
featured
at
<a
href="/wiki/Disneyland_Park_(Paris)?qsrc=3044"
title="Disneyland
Park
(Paris)">Euro
Disneyland</a>
after
that
park
opened
in
1992.
All
four
parks'
Captain
EO
installations
stayed
open
well
into
the
1990s:
the
Paris
installation
was
the
last
one
to
close,
in
1998.<sup
id="cite_ref-Nelson_George_overview_41_75-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Nelson_George_overview_41-75">[76]</a></sup>
The
attraction
would
later
return
to
Disneyland
in
2010
after
Jackson's
death.<sup
id="cite_ref-Captain_EO_is_Back_to_Change_the_World_.E2.80.93_Movies_News_at_IGN_76-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Captain_EO_is_Back_to_Change_the_World_.E2.80.93_Movies_News_at_IGN-76">[77]</a></sup></p>








<p>In
1987,
Jackson
disassociated
himself
from
the
<a
href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses?qsrc=3044"
title="Jehovah's
Witnesses">Jehovah's
Witnesses</a>,
in
response
to
their
disapproval
of
the
<i>Thriller</i>
video.<sup
id="cite_ref-ebony1_77-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-ebony1-77">[78]</a></sup>
With
the
industry
expecting
another
major
hit,
Jackson's
first
album
in
five
years,
<i><a
href="/wiki/Bad_(album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Bad
(album)">Bad</a></i>
(1987),
was
highly
anticipated.<sup
id="cite_ref-TIME2_78-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-TIME2-78">[79]</a></sup>
It
did
not
top
<i>Thriller</i>
as
a
commercial
or
artistic
triumph,
but
<i>Bad</i>
was
still
a
substantial
success
in
its
own
right.</p>








<p>The
<i><a
href="/wiki/Bad_(album)?qsrc=3044"
title="Bad
(album)">Bad</a></i>
album
spawned
seven
hit
singles
in
the
U.S.,
five
of
which
(&quot;<a
href="/wiki/I_Just_Can%27t_Stop_Loving_You?qsrc=3044"
title="I
Just
Can't
Stop
Loving
You">I
Just
Can't
Stop
Loving
You</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Bad_(Michael_Jackson_song)?qsrc=3044"
title="Bad
(Michael
Jackson
song)">Bad</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/The_Way_You_Make_Me_Feel?qsrc=3044"
title="The
Way
You
Make
Me
Feel">The
Way
You
Make
Me
Feel</a>&quot;,
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Man_in_the_Mirror?qsrc=3044"
title="Man
in
the
Mirror">Man
in
the
Mirror</a>&quot;
and
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Dirty_Diana?qsrc=3044"
title="Dirty
Diana">Dirty
Diana</a>&quot;)
reached
number
one
on
the
<i>Billboard</i>
Hot&nbsp;100
charts.
This
was
a
record
for
most
number
one
Hot
100
singles
from
any
one
album,
including
<i>Thriller.</i><sup
id="cite_ref-A_life_in_the_spotlight.E2.80.94cnn_79-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-A_life_in_the_spotlight.E2.80.94cnn-79">[80]</a></sup>
Although
the
title
track's
video
was
arguably
derivative
of
the
video
for
the
earlier
single
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Beat_It?qsrc=3044"
title="Beat
It">Beat
It</a>&quot;,
the
<a
href="/wiki/Bad_(Michael_Jackson_song)?qsrc=3044#Music_video"
title="Bad
(Michael
Jackson
song)">&quot;Bad&quot;
video</a>
still
proved
to
be
one
of
Jackson's
iconic
moments.
It
was
a
gritty
but
colorful
epic
set
against
the
backdrop
of
the
<a
href="/wiki/New_York_City_Subway?qsrc=3044"
title="New
York
City
Subway">New
York
City
Subway</a>
system,
with
costuming
and
choreography
inspired
by
<i><a
href="/wiki/West_Side_Story?qsrc=3044"
title="West
Side
Story">West
Side
Story</a>.</i>
As
of
2012,
the
album
sold
between
30
to
45
million
copies
worldwide.<sup
id="cite_ref-Telegraph_Bad_25_80-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Telegraph_Bad_25-80">[81]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-NME_Bad_81-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-NME_Bad-81">[82]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-MTV_Bad_82-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-MTV_Bad-82">[83]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Reuters_Bad_25_83-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Reuters_Bad_25-83">[84]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Chicago_Bad_25_84-0"
class="reference"><a

Thanks
to
the
<i>Bad</i>
album,
Bruce
Swedien
and
<a
href="/wiki/Humberto_Gatica?qsrc=3044"
title="Humberto
Gatica">Humberto
Gatica</a>
won
one
Grammy
in
1988
for
Best
Engineered
Recording&nbsp;–
Non
Classical
and
Michael
Jackson
won
one
Grammy
for
Best
Music
Video,
Short
Form
for
&quot;<a
href="/wiki/Leave_Me_Alone?qsrc=3044"
title="Leave
Me
Alone">Leave
Me
Alone</a>&quot;
in
1989.<sup
id="cite_ref-MJ_Grammy.27s_31-2"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-MJ_Grammy.27s-31">[32]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Bruce_Grammy.27s_38-1"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Bruce_Grammy.27s-38">[39]</a></sup>
In
the
same
year,
Jackson
won
an
Award
of
Achievement
at
the
American
Music
Awards
because
<i>Bad</i>
is
the
first
album
ever
to
generate
five
number
one
singles
in
the
U.S.,
the
first
album
to
top
in
25
countries
and
the
best-selling
album
worldwide
in
1987
and
in
1988.<sup
id="cite_ref-AMAs_1989_87-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-AMAs_1989-87">[88]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-AMAs_1989_2_88-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-AMAs_1989_2-88">[89]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-AMAs_1989_3_89-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-AMAs_1989_3-89">[90]</a></sup><sup
id="cite_ref-Bad_best_seller_90-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-Bad_best_seller-90">[91]</a></sup>
In
1988,
&quot;Bad&quot;
won
an
American
Music
Award
for
Favorite
Soul/R&amp;B
Single.<sup
id="cite_ref-AMAs_1988_91-0"
class="reference"><a
href="#cite_note-AMAs_1988-91">[92]</a></sup></p>

view:
Jackson
wearing
the
costume
on
a
June
2,

performance
in
Vienna,
